import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a depthwise-separable 2D convolution operation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.depthwise = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=in_channels, bias=bias)
        self.pointwise = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the depthwise-separable 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        x = self.depthwise(x)
        x = self.pointwise(x)
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 64
out_channels = 128
kernel_size = 3
width = 512
height = 512
stride = 1
padding = 1
dilation = 1

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward computation of a depthwise-separable 2D convolution: depthwise conv (groups=in_channels) followed by pointwise conv (1x1). The goal is speedup over PyTorch reference. First, let's understand what the model does: - Input: [B, C_in, H, W] - Depthwise conv: Conv2d with groups=C_in, kernel_size=K, stride=S, padding=P, dilation=D, bias maybe. Output: [B, C_in, H_out, W_out] where H_out = floor((H + 2*P - D*(K-1) - 1)/S) + 1. - Pointwise conv: Conv2d 1x1, stride=1, padding=0, same bias flag. Output: [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]. We need to fuse these two operations into a single kernel to avoid intermediate memory reads/writes and to maximize throughput. Given the test parameters: B=16, C_in=64, C_out=128, K=3, H=W=512, S=1, P=1, D=1, bias=False. So the depthwise conv is 3x3 with padding 1, stride 1, dilation 1. Output size remains 512x512. Then pointwise conv is 1x1 from 64 to 128 channels. The naive approach: launch a fused kernel where each thread computes one output pixel (or a tile) for all output channels. Given C_in=64, C_out=128, K=3, H=W=512, B=16. Total output elements: 16 * 128 * 512 * 512 = 536,870,912. If each thread computes one output element, we need ~537M threads, which is a lot. In CUDA, we can have up to ~2^31 threads per grid dimension? Actually max grid x dimension is 2^31-1. So it's okay. But each thread would compute a dot product over C_in * K * K + C_in (pointwise) operations. But wait, the fused kernel can compute output[B, oc, h, w] = sum_{ic, ky, kx} weight_dw[ic, ky, kx] * weight_pw[oc, ic] * x[B, ic, h*stride+ky*D-P, w*stride+kx*D-P] + bias terms. Since it's depthwise, each input channel has its own spatial filter. So the fused weight for each output channel oc and input spatial position is weight_pw[oc, ic] * weight_dw[ic, ky, kx]. So we can precompute a combined weight of shape [C_out, C_in, K, K] = 128*64*3*3 = 73,728 floats. That's tiny. Then the operation is just a standard convolution with this combined weight (groups=1, kernel KxK). Wait, is that correct? Let's verify: depthwise conv output y[ic, h, w] = sum_{ky,kx} w_dw[ic, ky, kx] * x[ic, h_s, w_s]. Then pointwise conv output z[oc, h, w] = sum_{ic} w_pw[oc, ic] * y[ic, h, w]. So z[oc, h, w] = sum_{ic, ky, kx} w_pw[oc, ic] * w_dw[ic, ky, kx] * x[ic, h_s, w_s]. Yes, that's exactly a standard convolution with weight w_comb[oc, ic, ky, kx] = w_pw[oc, ic] * w_dw[ic, ky, kx]. And bias: if bias is True, then depthwise has bias_dw[ic] and pointwise has bias_pw[oc]. The fused bias would be bias_pw[oc] + sum_{ic} w_pw[oc, ic] * bias_dw[ic]? Wait, no. Let's trace: y[ic, h, w] = sum_{ky,kx} w_dw[ic, ky, kx] * x[ic, h_s, w_s] + b_dw[ic]. z[oc, h, w] = sum_{ic} w_pw[oc, ic] * y[ic, h, w] + b_pw[oc] = sum_{ic} w_pw[oc, ic] * (sum_{ky,kx} w_dw[ic, ky, kx] * x[ic, h_s, w_s] + b_dw[ic]) + b_pw[oc] = sum_{ic, ky,kx} w_pw[oc, ic] * w_dw[ic, ky, kx] * x[ic, h_s, w_s] + sum_{ic} w_pw[oc, ic] * b_dw[ic] + b_pw[oc]. So the fused bias is b_comb[oc] = b_pw[oc] + sum_{ic} w_pw[oc, ic] * b_dw[ic]. We can precompute the combined weight and bias in the constructor or in the first forward call. Then the operation reduces to a standard conv2d with groups=1, kernel_size=K, stride=S, padding=P, dilation=D, in_channels=C_in, out_channels=C_out. But the standard PyTorch conv2d for these sizes might already be highly optimized (cuDNN). However, PyTorch uses two separate conv calls with intermediate memory. Fusing them into a single custom kernel may be faster because we eliminate the intermediate tensor and reduce memory bandwidth. But cuDNN might also fuse them implicitly? Usually not; depthwise separable is two separate cuDNN calls. A custom fused kernel can beat it, especially if we can exploit shared memory or keep everything in registers. However, writing a generic conv2d kernel that matches cuDNN performance is hard. But given the sizes, maybe a simple kernel that loads input into shared memory and computes the output can be competitive because of the fusion and because cuDNN overhead for two calls. We need to know the GPU specs. Let's call get_gpu_specs first.

reasoning
Let me analyze this problem. We need to implement a depthwise-separable 2D convolution using a custom CUDA kernel. A depthwise-separable convolution consists of: 1. Depthwise conv: groups=in_channels, so each input channel is convolved with its own kernel 2. Pointwise conv: 1x1 conv from in_channels to out_channels The test parameters are: - batch_size = 16 - in_channels = 64 - out_channels = 128 - kernel_size = 3 - width = 512 - height = 512 - stride = 1 - padding = 1 - dilation = 1 So the depthwise conv is: 64 input channels, 64 output channels (groups=64), 3x3 kernel, stride=1, padding=1, dilation=1 Output of depthwise: [16, 64, 512, 512] Then pointwise: 64 -> 128, 1x1 kernel Output: [16, 128, 512, 512] For a fused kernel, I need to combine both operations. The key insight is that we can load the intermediate values and immediately apply the pointwise convolution, avoiding writing the full intermediate depthwise output to global memory. For a custom CUDA kernel, I should think about how to optimize: 1. Fuse depthwise + pointwise to reduce memory bandwidth 2. Use shared memory for input tiles and register for weights 3. Block tiling strategy For a 512x512 image with 3x3 depthwise kernel and padding=1: - Output size: 512x512 - We need to handle batches and channels A good strategy: - Each block processes a tile of spatial locations (e.g., 16x16 or 32x8) - Block also processes multiple output channels from pointwise (e.g., 32 or 64) and multiple input channels/batch items Let me think about the exact design: - Input: [B, C, H, W] - Depthwise weight: [C, 1, K, K] - Pointwise weight: [out_C, in_C, 1, 1] - Bias (optional) For fusion: For each output position (b, oc, h, w): accum = 0 for ic in range(in_channels): dw_val = 0 for kh in range(K): for kw in range(K): ih = h * stride + kh * dilation - padding iw = w * stride + kw * dilation - padding if in bounds: dw_val += input[b, ic, ih, iw] * dw_weight[ic, 0, kh, kw] accum += dw_val * pw_weight[oc, ic, 0, 0] if bias: accum += bias[oc] output[b, oc, h, w] = accum This is compute-bound for large C, but let's check: - For each output pixel at [b, oc, h, w]: - in_C * K * K multiplies for depthwise - in_C multiplies for pointwise - Total: in_C * (K*K + 1) = 64 * 10 = 640 FMAs per output element - Output elements: B * out_C * H * W = 16 * 128 * 512 * 512 ≈ 537M - Total FMAs: 537M * 640 ≈ 344B FMAs = 688B ops (since FMA is 2 ops) - At 67 TFLOPS FP32, this would take ~10ms if compute bound - HBM bandwidth: 3.35 TB/s - Reading input: 16 * 64 * 512 * 512 * 4 bytes = 1GB - Reading weights: 64 * 1 * 3 * 3 * 4 + 128 * 64 * 1 * 1 * 4 = 2.3KB + 32.8KB = tiny - Writing output: 16 * 128 * 512 * 512 * 4 bytes = 2GB - Total memory if unfused: ~3GB minimum → ~1ms at bandwidth - But intermediate depthwise output: 16 * 64 * 512 * 512 * 4 = 1GB extra write + read So fusing saves ~2GB of memory traffic, which is crucial. For a fused kernel on H100, I should: 1. Tile in the spatial dimension using shared memory for input 2. Each thread block processes a batch of output channels (oc) 3. Each thread computes one or more output pixels for multiple output channels Actually, for better memory coalescing and utilization: - Process multiple output channels per block - Process spatial tiles per block - Use vectorized loads where possible Let me design a more specific kernel: Grid: [ceil(W/TX), ceil(H/TY), B * ceil(out_C/OC_TILE)] Block: [TX, TY, 1] or similar, but we need to be careful about register usage. Actually, since pointwise combines all input channels, we can't easily parallelize across input channels within a single output pixel without atomics or reduction. But we can: - Have each thread compute a partial sum over a subset of input channels, then reduce - Or have each thread compute the full output for a subset of output channels and spatial positions Let me think of a warp-specialized or cooperative approach: **Approach 1: Thread per output pixel, loop over input channels** - Each thread computes output[b, oc, h, w] for specific (b, h, w) and a range of oc - Block size: e.g. 256 threads - Each block handles: 256 output pixels across spatial dimensions, and OC_TILE output channels - For each input channel ic: - Load input pixel values for the 3x3 neighborhood for all threads (using shared memory) - Compute depthwise for this ic - Then multiply by pointwise weights for all oc - This way, input is loaded once per block and reused for all output channels This seems promising. Let me formalize: Block configuration: - BlockDim.x = TX * TY (e.g., 256 threads) - Spatial tile: TX=16, TY=16 = 256 pixels - OC_TILE output channels per block (e.g., 32) But we need to map thread index to (x, y) in the tile. For shared memory: - To compute TX * TY output pixels with 3x3 kernel and padding=1, we need (TX+2) * (TY+2) input pixels per channel - With TX=16, TY=16: 18*18 = 324 floats = ~1.3KB per channel - If we process one channel at a time in shared memory, this is very manageable Wait, but if we loop over channels with shared memory reload, the shared memory for input tile needs to be loaded for each channel. That's fine since we only need 1.3KB. **Proposed kernel design:** ``` #define TX 16 #define TY 16 #define OC_TILE 64 // Grid: (ceil(W/TX), ceil(H/TY), B * ceil(out_C/OC_TILE)) // Block: (TX * TY, 1, 1) // Shared mem: float s_in[(TX+2)*(TY+2)] - for current input channel tile Each block: b = blockIdx.z / occ oc_base = (blockIdx.z % occ) * OC_TILE For oc in 0..OC_TILE-1: accum[oc] = 0.0f For ic in 0..in_channels: // Load input tile for channel ic into shared memory // Compute depthwise conv for this ic across all spatial positions in tile // Then multiply by pointwise weights and accumulate // Actually, each thread handles one spatial position // Thread tid -> (tx, ty) tx = tid % TX, ty = tid / TX // For this (tx, ty), compute depthwise result using shared memory dw = 0 for kh in 0..2: for kw in 0..2: ih = ty + kh, iw = tx + kw // in shared mem coords dw += s_in[ih*(TX+2)+iw] * dw_weight[ic, kh, kw] // Now for each oc in 0..OC_TILE-1: for oc in 0..OC_TILE-1: accum[oc] += dw * pw_weight[oc_base + oc, ic] // Write outputs for oc in 0..OC_TILE-1: if in bounds: out[b, oc_base+oc, h, w] = accum[oc] + bias ``` But keeping OC_TILE accumulators in registers per thread can be expensive. With OC_TILE=32, each thread needs 32 registers just for accumulators. With 256 threads, each block would need high register pressure. Alternative: Each block processes fewer spatial pixels but more output channels, or vice versa. Actually, let me reconsider. We can have each warp handle a subset of output channels, and all warps share the loaded input tile. **Better design:** - Block: (TX, TY, 1) where TX*TY <= 1024 - Each thread handles one spatial position and OC_TILE output channels - OC_TILE can be small, like 4 or 8, to keep register count reasonable - Grid: (ceil(W/TX), ceil(H/TY), B * out_C / OC_TILE) — no, this doesn't work because all input channels must contribute. Actually, the loop over input channels means all output channels need all input channels. So we can't split input channels across blocks without a reduction. Let me think about this differently. Given that in_C=64, out_C=128, we could have blocks each process all input channels but only a subset of output channels. **Revised design:** - Block size: 256 threads - Each block processes 1 batch item, 1 spatial tile, and OC_TILE output channels - OC_TILE = 32 or 64 - Each thread computes all spatial positions in the tile? No... Actually, let's use a 2D thread block for spatial positions and have each thread handle multiple output channels. But the number of output channels is 128, so we could have: - Each block has 256 threads - Each block handles a 16x16 spatial tile (256 pixels) - Each block handles all 128 output channels... no wait, if each thread handles 1 pixel and all 128 output channels, that's 128 accumulators per thread, which is too many registers. OK, let's try: - Block: 16x16 = 256 threads for spatial tile - Each block handles OC_TILE output channels (e.g., OC_TILE = 8 or 16) - Then we need 128/8 = 16 blocks per spatial tile and batch - Total blocks: (W/16) * (H/16) * B * (out_C/OC_TILE) = 32 * 32 * 16 * 16 = 262,144 blocks - That's a lot of blocks but should be fine for occupancy Wait, but if different blocks handle different output channels for the same spatial position, each block loads the same input tile. This means we load input in_C times per spatial tile per batch, and if we have out_C/OC_TILE blocks for the same tile, we load input (out_C/OC_TILE) times more than necessary. So for better memory efficiency, we should have each block handle more output channels to reuse input loads. But registers limit this. With TX=16, TY=16, we need 18*18*4 = 1296 bytes of shared memory per input channel. If we process 1 channel at a time in a loop, memory for shared mem is very small. If OC_TILE=32 and we have 256 threads, each thread needs 32 accumulators. At 4 bytes each = 128 bytes. With 255 registers max, that's 32 registers per thread for accumulators. Plus other registers, should be OK. Actually, 32 accumulators of float is exactly 32 registers. Plus input loads, indices, etc, might be around 50-60 registers total. That's fine. Block size 256 * 60 registers = 15,360 registers per block. With 64K per SM, that's about 4 blocks per SM. Good. **Refined kernel:** ``` #define TILE_X 16 #define TILE_Y 16 #define OC_TILE 32 __global__ void fused_dw_pw_conv( const float* __restrict__ input, // [B, in_C, H, W] const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, // [in_C, 1, K, K] const float* __restrict__ pw_weight, // [out_C, in_C, 1, 1] const float* __restrict__ bias, // [out_C] or nullptr float* __restrict__ output, // [B, out_C, H, W] int B, int in_C, int out_C, int H, int W, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { // Block: (TILE_X, TILE_Y, 1) int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int bx = blockIdx.x; // W tile int by = blockIdx.y; // H tile int bz = blockIdx.z; // b * n_oc_tiles + oc_tile_idx int b = bz / ((out_C + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE); int oc_tile_idx = bz % ((out_C + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE); int oc_base = oc_tile_idx * OC_TILE; int w_out = bx * TILE_X + tx; int h_out = by * TILE_Y + ty; float accum[OC_TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f; __shared__ float s_in[(TILE_Y + 2) * (TILE_X + 2)]; // for K=3, pad=1 for (int ic = 0; ic < in_C; ic++) { // Load input tile into shared memory // We need to load (TILE_Y+2) * (TILE_X+2) elements // With TILE_X*TILE_Y = 256 threads // Can do this cooperatively int smem_size = (TILE_Y + 2) * (TILE_X + 2); // Each thread loads multiple elements for (int idx = ty * TILE_X + tx; idx < smem_size; idx += TILE_X * TILE_Y) { int sy = idx / (TILE_X + 2); int sx = idx % (TILE_X + 2); int ih = by * TILE_Y + sy - padding; int iw = bx * TILE_X + sx - padding; float val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { val = input[((b * in_C + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } s_in[sy * (TILE_X + 2) + sx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Only compute if this thread's output is in bounds if (h_out < H && w_out < W) { // Compute depthwise for this pixel #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { float in_val = s_in[(ty + kh) * (TILE_X + 2) + (tx + kw)]; float w_val = dw_weight[(ic * 1 + 0) * K * K + kh * K + kw]; float dw = in_val * w_val; // Pointwise accumulation #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { int oc = oc_base + i; if (oc < out_C) { float pw_w = pw_weight[(oc * in_C + ic)]; accum[i] += dw * pw_w; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output if (h_out < H && w_out < W) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { int oc = oc_base + i; if (oc < out_C) { if (bias != nullptr) accum[i] += bias[oc]; output[((b * out_C + oc) * H + h_out) * W + w_out] = accum[i]; } } } } ``` Wait, there are some issues: 1. The inner loop over kh, kw does K*K iterations, and inside we loop over OC_TILE. For K=3 and OC_TILE=32, that's 9*32=288 FMAs in the inner body. With `#pragma unroll`, the compiler should handle it. 2. But loading `pw_weight` inside kh, kw loops means we load the same weights repeatedly. Better to load them once per ic... but there are 32 of them, so we'd need to load 32 floats into registers. Let me optimize: - Load all pw weights for this block's OC_TILE into registers (or shared memory) once per ic - Actually, pw_weight for oc_tile_idx and ic: we can load `pw_w[OC_TILE]` per thread, but that's 32 floats per thread per ic, which is 32 loads. Total loads: in_C * OC_TILE = 64 * 32 = 2048 per thread, which is fine. Actually, even better: we can restructure so the pointwise weights are loaded once and reused across kh, kw. ``` float pw_w_reg[OC_TILE]; for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { int oc = oc_base + i; if (oc < out_C) { pw_w_reg[i] = pw_weight[oc * in_C + ic]; } } #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { float in_val = s_in[...]; float dw_w = dw_weight[ic * K * K + kh * K + kw]; float dw = in_val * dw_w; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += dw * pw_w_reg[i]; } } } ``` This reduces redundant global memory loads for pointwise weights. Each pointwise weight is loaded exactly once per thread per ic. However, the real issue might be register pressure with OC_TILE=32. Let me check if OC_TILE=16 works better. With OC_TILE=16: - Accumulators: 16 registers - pw_w_reg: 16 registers - Other: ~10 registers - Total: ~42 registers per thread. Very comfortable. But with OC_TILE=16, for out_C=128, we need 8 blocks per spatial tile. Total blocks: 32*32*16*8 = 131,072. Still plenty. But wait, each block loads input from global memory for all in_C channels. So the total HBM reads per spatial tile would be: - Input: B * in_C * H * W * 4 bytes (same, read once by all blocks for that tile) - Actually, no. If multiple blocks process the same spatial tile but different oc, each block reads the SAME input. So total input reads = (out_C / OC_TILE) * input size. With OC_TILE=16, input is read 8 times. With OC_TILE=32, input is read 4 times. With OC_TILE=64, input is read 2 times. With OC_TILE=128, input is read 1 time. So larger OC_TILE is better for bandwidth, but higher register pressure. What if we process multiple input channels per shared memory load? Like process a group of input channels together. For instance, load IC_TILE=2 input channels into shared memory. SMEM for 2 channels: 2*18*18*4 = 2.6KB, fine. But each channel has different depthwise weights, so we'd need to load those too. Best approach: OC_TILE=64. Let me check register pressure: - Accumulators: 64 floats = 64 registers - pw_w_reg: 64 floats = 64 registers - That's 128 registers already. Plus loop vars, pointers, etc. Could exceed 255 but might be tight. Actually, we don't strictly need pw_w_reg if we load from constant/shared memory. We can put pw_weight in shared memory! Or in constant memory if we use__constant__. But pw_weight per block per ic is small: 64 floats * 4 bytes = 256 bytes. Very small. We could load it into shared memory once per block per ic, and all threads read from there. **Even better design:** - Use shared memory for input tile - Use shared memory for pointwise weights for the current OC_TILE - Block processes 1 (or more) input channels at a time Shared memory per block: - Input tile: (TILE_Y+2) * (TILE_X+2) * sizeof(float) = 324 * 4 = 1,296 bytes - PW weights: OC_TILE * sizeof(float) = 64 * 4 = 256 bytes - Total: ~1.6KB per block. Very small. So we can have a large number of blocks resident on each SM. Revised kernel with OC_TILE=64 and smem for pw weights: ``` #define TX 16 #define TY 16 #define OC_TILE 64 __global__ void kernel(...) { __shared__ float s_in[(TY+2)*(TX+2)]; __shared__ float s_pw[OC_TILE]; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_C; ic++) { // Load input ... // Load pw weights for this ic for (int idx = ty * TX + tx; idx < OC_TILE; idx += TX * TY) { int oc = oc_base + idx; if (oc < out_C) { s_pw[idx] = pw_weight[oc * in_C + ic]; } else { s_pw[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (in_bounds) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { float dw = s_in[...] * dw_weight[...]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += dw * s_pw[i]; } } } } __syncthreads(); } } ``` But reading `s_pw[i]` from shared memory in the innermost loop isn't optimal if i is looped over with many iterations. It would be 64 shared memory reads per inner iteration. SMEM bandwidth is much higher than HBM but still, for 64*9=576 reads per thread per ic, with 256 threads, that's 147K reads per block per ic. At 64 ic, that's ~9.4M reads per block. At 3.35TB/s HBM but much higher SMEM bandwidth, it might be fine. But wait, we can have each thread only compute a subset of the OC_TILE, to reduce the iterations per thread. For instance: - Divide OC_TILE among warps or use multiple registers. Actually, another approach: since the pointwise weights are just 64 floats that are the same for all threads in the block, they could be broadcast from shared memory to all threads simultaneously. On H100, shared memory can service many broadcasts per cycle. But a bigger issue is the 64 accumulators. 64 floats = 64 registers. That's manageable. Let me think about what register pressure the compiler sees: - 64 accumulators - ~5 loop induction variables - 2 pointers - 4-8 temporaries Total: ~75-80 registers. Well under 255. Actually, `#pragma unroll` on the OC_TILE loop with 64 iterations and 64 accumulators... the compiler might struggle or use more registers. But we can help by making it a loop with `#pragma unroll`. Hmm, actually if we load `s_pw[i]` in the inner loop, the compiler might keep the array in registers anyway after the first read (cached). But shared memory reads have ~20 cycle latency, so we might want to prefetch or rely on L1 cache. An alternative is to have each thread handle a smaller set of output channels. For example, with a block of 256 threads, we could have: - Threads 0..63 handle oc_base + 0..7 for their pixel - Threads 64..127 handle oc_base + 8..15 - etc. No wait, that complicates things because each thread needs to load the input tile anyway. Let me structure the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' template <int K, int OC_TILE> __global__ void fused_conv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ pw_weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int in_C, int out_C, int H, int W, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { // ... } ''' # We need a wrapper function for each common K, or a generic one ``` Actually, we can have a host-side function that takes all parameters and has a switch for K: ```cpp void fused_conv_forward( const float* input, const float* dw_weight, const float* pw_weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int in_C, int out_C, int H, int W, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { // dispatch based on K } ``` But then the kernel inside doesn't have K as a template argument. To fix this, I can use a template dispatch helper: ```cpp template <int OC_TILE> void dispatch_k(int K, ...) { dim3 block(TX, TY); dim3 grid(...); switch (K) { case 1: fused_conv_kernel<1, OC_TILE><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 3: fused_conv_kernel<3, OC_TILE><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 5: fused_conv_kernel<5, OC_TILE><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; default: ... // generic or error } } ``` Wait, this works! Wait, but 128 accumulators and the compiler unrolling kh,kw loops and the i loop... the instruction count per iteration becomes large. 128 FMAs in the inner loop with a dw value loaded from shared memory. That means each thread issues 128 FMA instructions sequentially. On H100, each SM can issue many FMAs per cycle, but per thread it's limited. Still, with 256 threads per block, there should be enough instruction-level parallelism across warps. Actually, in CUDA, threads within a warp execute in lockstep. If each thread has 128 FMAs to perform with the same shared memory broadcast input (dw), they all do it together. The shared memory broadcast can feed multiple threads. FMA throughput is 1 per clock per thread (on modern GPUs), so 128 FMAs take 128 clocks + latency. The GPU has many warps to hide this latency. With 256 threads = 8 warps, if each warp takes ~150 cycles for the inner kh,kw body, and there are 9 iterations, that's 1350 cycles per ic. With 64 ic = 86,400 cycles per thread. At 1 GHz, that's 86 microseconds. But wait, shared memory latency is ~20 cycles. FMA latency is ~4 cycles. In a loop of 128 FMAs using the same dw but different pw weights, since accum[i] and s_pw[i] are independent, the compiler should pipeline these nicely. Actually, I realize: `s_pw[i]` is different for each i. So each thread does: ``` accum[0] += dw * s_pw[0]; accum[1] += dw * s_pw[1]; ... accum[127] += dw * s_pw[127]; ``` Since accum[i] is only used once per iteration, these are dependent chains of length 1. The compiler can interleave instructions for different i to hide latency. With 128 independent accumulators, there's plenty of instruction-level parallelism! However, loading s_pw[0..127] from shared memory: if we keep s_pw in shared memory, each access has ~20 cycle latency. But 128 accesses with 20 cycle latency means we need at least 128/20 ≈ 6 warps to hide latency, or rely on L1 cache. We have 8 warps, so we're close. Alternatively, we could load s_pw into registers first: ``` float pw_reg[OC_TILE]; for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) pw_reg[i] = s_pw[i]; ``` Then use pw_reg in the inner loop. But that costs 128 registers for pw_reg, which we might not have if we already use 128 for accum. 128 + 128 = 256 registers, which exceeds 255. So we can't do both. Solution: use a smaller OC_TILE where we CAN load pw into registers. With OC_TILE=64: - accumulators: 64 registers - pw_reg: 64 registers - total: 128 registers + overhead. Should be OK. But then we double the number of blocks and double input reads. Hmm. Trade-off: - OC_TILE=128: More register pressure, fewer blocks, less memory traffic, possible register spilling - OC_TILE=64: Moderate registers, more blocks, 2x memory traffic, smoother execution Given that H100 has excellent memory bandwidth and the compute is substantial, OC_TILE=64 might give better overall occupancy and latency hiding. Let me try OC_TILE=64 first. Actually, a compromise: OC_TILE=64 but process IC_TILE=2 input channels at once, reusing the OC_TILE accumulators. For 2 input channels, we need 2 * (TY+2)*(TX+2) floats in shared memory. That's still small. Or OC_TILE=32 with IC_TILE=2? OC_TILE=32 gives very comfortable register usage. Let me go with a conservative OC_TILE=32 or OC_TILE=64. Let me try OC_TILE=64. Wait, I just realized another optimization. Since the depthwise and pointwise weights are constant during inference, and the kernel is compiled when weights are known... no, the kernel is compiled at module load time, before weights are assigned. So we can't embed weights in the kernel. However, I can use `__constant__` memory for small weights. Depthwise weights: in_C * K * K = 64 * 9 = 576 floats = 2.3KB. Pointwise weights: 128 * 64 = 8192 floats = 32KB. Total: ~35KB. That's within the 64KB constant memory limit! Using constant memory for weights could dramatically speed up weight loads because: 1. Constant memory is cached with a dedicated cache 2. All threads in a warp reading the same address get broadcast (which happens for dw_weight[ic, kh, kw] and s_pw[i] if we put pw in constant) But constant memory requires compile-time size or copying at runtime. Since the sizes are dynamic, we'd need to copy to constant memory on each kernel launch or once at module load. The latter is tricky with `load_inline`. But we can declare `__constant__ float c_dw[1024*9];` and copy weights into it before launching the kernel. Actually, using `cudaMemcpyToSymbol` from Python is possible but adds overhead. And constant memory size needs to be declared. For up to reasonable channel counts, we could declare a maximum size. An alternative is to use `__restrict__` on the weight pointers, which helps the compiler know there are no aliasing issues. But constant memory is specifically optimized for broadcast. For simplicity, I'll start with global memory with `__restrict__` and see how fast it is. If needed, we can add constant memory. There's also texture memory / bindless textures, but that's outdated. Another optimization: use warp-level primitives for the pointwise if we split oc across warps. For example, have 4 warps each handle 32 ocs. But then each warp needs its own set of accumulators. This is what the natural thread-level accumulation already does. Let me think about a different organization entirely: **im2col implicit gemm for pointwise, with depthwise fused in the im2col generation.** For pointwise conv (1x1), the operation is GEMM: [out_C, in_C] @ [in_C, N*H*W]. The "activation matrix" for pointwise is exactly the output of depthwise conv. If we generate the depthwise output on-the-fly and feed it into a GEMM, that's the fused approach. Since pointwise is a GEMM, we could theoretically use CUTLASS or cuBLAS with a custom iterator that generates the depthwise output. But that's very complex. Instead, consider doing the pointwise as a GEMM using shared memory: - Load a block of [OC_TILE, IC_TILE] from pw_weight into shared memory - Load a block of [IC_TILE, spatial] from depthwise output (computed on-the-fly) into shared memory - Compute the matrix multiplication For depthwise output computation on-the-fly: - For each spatial position in the tile, and for each ic in IC_TILE, compute the depthwise value from input - Store in shared memory or registers - Then do the GEMM This is basically aGEMM with custom A matrix generation. With H100's fast tensor cores, even without tensor cores, this could be efficient. A simpler version without explicit GEMM abstractions: - Block size: e.g., 128 threads - Each warp handles 32 output channels (one warp per 32 ocs) - 4 warps handle 128 ocs - For each spatial tile (e.g., 16x8 = 128 pixels): - Load input tile for all IC_TILE=8 channels into shared memory - For each spatial position, each warp computes 32 depthwise values (one per ic) and then does 32 * 8 FMAs for pointwise Actually, this starts getting complicated. Let me stick with the simpler per-thread pixel approach and tune OC_TILE. Let me now write the kernel and test it. Actually, I want to think about vectorized memory accesses. For loading input into shared memory, can we use float4? For TILE_X=32, each row in shared memory has 34 elements. Not divisible by 4. So vectorization might be messy. What if TILE_X=28 so TX+2=30 (not divisible by 4)? Or TILE_X=30 so TX+2=32 (divisible by 4!)? TILE_X=30: W/30 = 512/30 = 18 tiles, but 18*30=540 > 512. We'd have boundary checks. Actually, we handle boundary checks anyway. Or TILE_X=32 and just pad the shared memory to 36 (divisible by 4)? We can allocate 36*10 = 360 floats and only use 34 per row. Let me draft the code now. ```python cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define TILE_X 32 #define TILE_Y 8 template <int K, int OC_TILE> __global__ void fused_conv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ pw_weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int in_C, int out_C, int H, int W, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { const int tx = threadIdx.x; const int ty = threadIdx.y; const int bx = blockIdx.x; const int by = blockIdx.y; const int bz = blockIdx.z; const int n_oc_tiles = (out_C + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE; const int b_idx = bz / n_oc_tiles; const int oc_tile_idx = bz % n_oc_tiles; const int oc_base = oc_tile_idx * OC_TILE; const int w_out = bx * TILE_X + tx; const int h_out = by * TILE_Y + ty; const bool in_bounds = (h_out < H) && (w_out < W); const int smem_w = TILE_X + (K - 1) * dilation; // Actually for K=3,pad=1,dil=1: needs TILE_X+2 const int smem_h = TILE_Y + (K - 1) * dilation; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_in = s_mem; float accum[OC_TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] = 0.0f; } const int in_plane_size = H * W; const int out_plane_size = H * W; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_C; ic++) { // Load input tile into shared memory const float* in_ptr = input + (b_idx * in_C + ic) * in_plane_size; const int total_smem = smem_h * smem_w; int idx = ty * TILE_X + tx; while (idx < total_smem) { int sy = idx / smem_w; int sx = idx % smem_w; int ih = by * TILE_Y + sy - padding; int iw = bx * TILE_X + sx - padding; float val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { val = in_ptr[ih * W + iw]; } s_in[idx] = val; idx += TILE_X * TILE_Y; } __syncthreads(); if (in_bounds) { // Load pointwise weights for this ic into registers float pw_reg[OC_TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { int oc = oc_base + i; if (oc < out_C) { pw_reg[i] = pw_weight[oc * in_C + ic]; } else { pw_reg[i] = 0.0f; } } // Compute depthwise and pointwise #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int sy = ty + kh * dilation; int sx = tx + kw * dilation; float in_val = s_in[sy * smem_w + sx]; float dw_val = dw_weight[ic * K * K + kh * K + kw]; float prod = in_val * dw_val; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += prod * pw_reg[i]; } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (in_bounds) { float* out_ptr = output + (b_idx * out_C + oc_base) * out_plane_size; } } } } """ ``` Hmm, I see a potential issue with `smem_w` and `smem_h`. The total effect is: output = pointwise(depthwise(input) + dw_bias) + pw_bias = pointwise(depthwise(input)) + pointwise(dw_bias) + pw_bias Where `pointwise(dw_bias)` is a 1x1 conv applied to `dw_bias` viewed as [B, in_C, H, W] where all spatial positions have the same value. This way, weight copying works exactly the same as the reference. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.depthwise = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=in_channels, bias=bias) self.pointwise = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias) self.has_bias = bias def forward(self, x): # Extract weights dw_weight = self.depthwise.weight # [in_C, 1, K, K] pw_weight = self.pointwise.weight # [out_C, in_C, 1, 1] if self.has_bias: dw_bias = self.depthwise.bias # [in_C] pw_bias = self.pointwise.bias # [out_C] # Compute combined bias # combined_bias[oc] = sum_ic dw_bias[ic] * pw_weight[oc, ic, 0, 0] + pw_bias[oc] # pw_weight shape: [out_C, in_C, 1, 1] # We can use einsum or reshape + matmul combined_bias = torch.matmul(pw_weight.squeeze(-1).squeeze(-1), dw_bias.unsqueeze(1)).squeeze(1) + pw_bias else: combined_bias = None return fused_conv_forward(x, dw_weight, pw_weight, combined_bias, self.depthwise.stride[0], self.depthwise.padding[0], self.depthwise.dilation[0]) ``` Wait, `self.depthwise.stride` is a tuple `(stride, stride)`. Let me write the complete code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define TILE_X 32 #define TILE_Y 8 template <int K, int OC_TILE> __global__ void fused_conv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ dw_weight, const float* __restrict__ pw_weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int in_C, int out_C, int H, int W, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { const int tx = threadIdx.x; const int ty = threadIdx.y; const int bx = blockIdx.x; const int by = blockIdx.y; const int bz = blockIdx.z; const int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; const int W_out = (W + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; const int n_oc_tiles = (out_C + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE; const int b_idx = bz / n_oc_tiles; const int oc_tile_idx = bz % n_oc_tiles; const int oc_base = oc_tile_idx * OC_TILE; const int w_out = bx * TILE_X + tx; const int h_out = by * TILE_Y + ty; const bool in_bounds = (h_out < H_out) && (w_out < W_out); const int smem_h = (TILE_Y - 1) * stride + (K - 1) * dilation + 1; const int smem_w = (TILE_X - 1) * stride + (K - 1) * dilation + 1; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_in = s_mem; float accum[OC_TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] = 0.0f; } const int in_plane_size = H * W; const int out_plane_size = H_out * W_out; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_C; ic++) { const float* in_ptr = input + (b_idx * in_C + ic) * in_plane_size; const int total_smem = smem_h * smem_w; int idx = ty * TILE_X + tx; while (idx < total_smem) { int sy = idx / smem_w; int sx = idx % smem_w; int ih = by * TILE_Y * stride + sy - padding; int iw = bx * TILE_X * stride + sx - padding; float val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { val = in_ptr[ih * W + iw]; } s_in[idx] = val; idx += TILE_X * TILE_Y; } __syncthreads(); if (in_bounds) { float pw_reg[OC_TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { int oc = oc_base + i; if (oc < out_C) { pw_reg[i] = pw_weight[oc * in_C + ic]; } else { pw_reg[i] = 0.0f; } } #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int sy = ty * stride + kh * dilation; int sx = tx * stride + kw * dilation; float in_val = s_in[sy * smem_w + sx]; float dw_val = dw_weight[ic * K * K + kh * K + kw]; float prod = in_val * dw_val; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += prod * pw_reg[i]; } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (in_bounds) { float* out_ptr = output + (b_idx * out_C + oc_base) * out_plane_size; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { int oc = oc_base + i; if (oc < out_C) { float val = accum[i]; if (bias != nullptr) { val += bias[oc]; } out_ptr[i * out_plane_size + h_out * W_out + w_out] = val; } } } } template <int K, int OC_TILE> void launch_fused_conv( int B, int in_C, int out_C, int H, int W, int stride, int padding, int dilation, const float* input, const float* dw_weight, const float* pw_weight, const float* bias, float* output ) { int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; dim3 block(TILE_X, TILE_Y); dim3 grid( (W_out + TILE_X - 1) / TILE_X, (H_out + TILE_Y - 1) / TILE_Y, B * ((out_C + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE) ); size_t smem_size = ((TILE_Y - 1) * stride + (K - 1) * dilation + 1) * ((TILE_X - 1) * stride + (K - 1) * dilation + 1) * sizeof(float); fused_conv_kernel<K, OC_TILE><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( input, dw_weight, pw_weight, bias, output, B, in_C, out_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation ); } template <int K> void dispatch_oc( int out_C, int B, int in_C, int H, int W, int stride, int padding, int dilation, const float* input, const float* dw_weight, const float* pw_weight, const float* bias, float* output ) { if (out_C <= 32) { launch_fused_conv<K, 32>(B, in_C, out_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input, dw_weight, pw_weight, bias, output); } else if (out_C <= 64) { launch_fused_conv<K, 64>(B, in_C, out_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input, dw_weight, pw_weight, bias, output); } else { launch_fused_conv<K, 128>(B, in_C, out_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input, dw_weight, pw_weight, bias, output); } } torch::Tensor fused_conv_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor dw_weight, torch::Tensor pw_weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { int B = input.size(0); int in_C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int out_C = pw_weight.size(0); int K = dw_weight.size(2); int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; auto output = torch::empty({B, out_C, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); const float* bias_ptr = nullptr; if (bias.has_value()) { bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>(); } const float* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<float>(); const float* dw_ptr = dw_weight.data_ptr<float>(); const float* pw_ptr = pw_weight.data_ptr<float>(); float* out_ptr = output.data_ptr<float>(); switch (K) { case 1: dispatch_oc<1>(out_C, B, in_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input_ptr, dw_ptr, pw_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr); break; case 3: dispatch_oc<3>(out_C, B, in_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input_ptr, dw_ptr, pw_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr); break; case 5: dispatch_oc<5>(out_C, B, in_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input_ptr, dw_ptr, pw_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr); break; case 7: dispatch_oc<7>(out_C, B, in_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input_ptr, dw_ptr, pw_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr); break; default: dispatch_oc<3>(out_C, B, in_C, H, W, stride, padding, dilation, input_ptr, dw_ptr, pw_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr); break; } return output; } ''' # Compile the extension fused_conv = load_inline( name='fused_conv', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['fused_conv_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=True ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.depthwise = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=in_channels, bias=bias) self.pointwise = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias) self.has_bias = bias def forward(self, x): # Ensure float32 and contiguous x = x.contiguous().float() dw_weight = self.depthwise.weight.contiguous() pw_weight = self.pointwise.weight.contiguous() bias = None if self.has_bias: dw_bias = self.depthwise.bias pw_bias = self.pointwise.bias # combined_bias = pw_weight @ dw_bias + pw_bias # pw_weight is [out_C, in_C, 1, 1] pw_mat = pw_weight.squeeze(-1).squeeze(-1) # [out_C, in_C] combined_bias = torch.matmul(pw_mat, dw_bias.unsqueeze(1)).squeeze(1) + pw_bias bias = combined_bias.contiguous() stride = self.depthwise.stride[0] padding = self.depthwise.padding[0] dilation = self.depthwise.dilation[0] return fused_conv.fused_conv_forward(x, dw_weight, pw_weight, bias, stride, padding, dilation) ``` Wait, I need to check: `nn.Conv2d` with `stride=1` passed as int. For out_C=128 and OC_TILE=128, all accumulators are used. Perfect. But with OC_TILE=128 and register pressure, the compiler might spill registers to local memory. Local memory is cached in L1, but spilling is still bad for performance. If we get register spills, we might need to use OC_TILE=64. Let me provide both and see which is faster. Actually, I think I should try OC_TILE=64 first. 64 accumulators + 64 pw_reg = 128 registers. With other variables, maybe 140. This is well under 255 and should not spill. With OC_TILE=128: 128 accum + 128 pw_reg = 256 registers. This is AT the 255 limit and will almost certainly cause spilling. The compiler might also fail to allocate all 128 pw_reg in registers and keep some in local memory. Wait, in my kernel, pw_reg is declared inside the IC loop. The compiler might keep it in registers if it decides to unroll the i loop. But it could also spill. To be safe, I should try OC_TILE=64 first. With out_C=128, 2 blocks per spatial location. Input read twice. But what if I put the pw weights in shared memory instead of registers? Then pw_reg isn't needed. Each thread just reads from shared memory: ```cpp __shared__ float s_pw[OC_TILE]; // load once per block per ic ... for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += prod * s_pw[i]; } ``` With OC_TILE=128, s_pw is 512 bytes in shared memory. Each thread still needs 128 accumulators. That's 128 registers per thread. Much better! And shared memory bandwidth is very high. Then for OC_TILE=128, register usage is ~128 accum + ~20 temps = ~150 registers. No spilling! And reading `s_pw[i]` from shared memory: with 256 threads, all accessing s_pw[i] sequentially in i. Each thread reads 128 shared memory locations. With shared memory latency of ~20 cycles, and 8 warps, the SM should hide this latency well. Also, each thread's reads are to the same shared memory locations but at different times. There's no bank conflict because all threads read different i in the loop (i is the same across threads in lockstep). Wait: All threads in a warp execute: ``` accum[0] += prod * s_pw[0]; accum[1] += prod * s_pw[1]; ... ``` At the same time, all threads in a warp access `s_pw[0]`. This is a broadcast! No bank conflict! Perfect. Then `accum[1]` access is different for each thread, but that's register access (no bank conflict). Then next iteration i=1: all threads access `s_pw[1]`. Another broadcast. This is EXCELLENT for shared memory! No bank conflicts at all. So using OC_TILE=128 with pw weights in shared memory is extremely efficient. Wait, but `s_pw` needs to be loaded by the block. For 128 floats, with 256 threads, we can have a few threads load them. That's completely parallel and fast. Let me redesign with OC_TILE=128 and pw weights in shared memory: ```cpp template <int K, int OC_TILE> __global__ void fused_conv_kernel(...) { // ... const int smem_h = ...; const int smem_w = ...; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_in = s_mem; float* s_pw = s_mem + smem_h * smem_w; // pw weights after input tile float accum[OC_TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_C; ic++) { // load input // ... // load pw weights for this ic // Only OC_TILE elements, use a subset of threads if (ty * TILE_X + tx < OC_TILE) { int oc = oc_base + (ty * TILE_X + tx); if (oc < out_C) { s_pw[ty * TILE_X + tx] = pw_weight[oc * in_C + ic]; } else { s_pw[ty * TILE_X + tx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (in_bounds) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { float prod = s_in[...] * dw_weight[...]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += prod * s_pw[i]; } } } } __syncthreads(); } // ... } ``` Wait, I need to make sure the total shared memory size is allocated correctly. In `launch_fused_conv`: ```cpp size_t smem_size = (smem_h * smem_w + OC_TILE) * sizeof(float); ``` And in the kernel, `s_pw = s_mem + smem_h * smem_w;`. For NVCC, it's usually fine for small constant loops. But 128 is large. If the compiler refuses to unroll, the loop might not be unrolled, which hurts performance because `accum[i]` accesses become dynamic array indexing (stored in local memory). Dynamic indexing into `float accum[128]` causes local memory usage! That's critical. If the loop over i is not unrolled, the compiler puts the `accum` array in local memory (spills). Local memory has ~100+ cycle latency. To prevent this, we NEED the loop to be unrolled. `#pragma unroll` should force it, but if register pressure is too high, the compiler might still spill. With OC_TILE=128: - 128 accumulators in registers (or local mem if spilled) - No pw_reg (uses shared memory) - This means we only need ~128 + 20 = ~148 registers. Should fit easily. The compiler should be able to fully unroll the 128-iteration loop with `#pragma unroll` without spilling. Actually, let me check: on modern GPUs, local memory is cached in L1, and a 128-float local memory array is 512 bytes. That's small. Even if it goes to local memory, it might stay in L1 cache. But it would still be slower than registers. To ensure registers, we could use a smaller OC_TILE=64. That gives us more confidence about register allocation. But with 128 floats per thread and 256 threads, we have 32K floats total per block. The SM register file is 64K registers total. At 64 threads per block (warp), 8 warps, 256 threads. Each thread can use up to 255 registers = 255 * 4 = 1020 bytes. 128 floats = 512 bytes. That's exactly 128 registers. Well within limits. Wait, I need to be more careful. The compiler may not allocate `s_pw` in registers because it's accessed via `s_pw[i]` where `i` is a constant in each unrolled iteration. The compiler optimizes this to direct register access only if `i` is a compile-time constant. That requires full unrolling. With #pragma unroll 128, it should work. But there's a subtlety: `pw_weight` loading into `s_pw` is done before the kh,kw loop. So `s_pw` is in shared memory, not registers. Then in the inner loop: ```cpp for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) { accum[i] += prod * s_pw[i]; } ``` With `#pragma unroll`, the compiler sees: ```cpp accum[0] += prod * s_pw[0]; accum[1] += prod * s_pw[1]; ... accum[127] += prod * s_pw[127]; ``` For each line, `s_pw[0]` is a shared memory load, `accum[0]` is a register, `prod` is a register. This is a FMA plus a shared memory load. Since all threads in the warp hit `s_pw[0]` simultaneously (broadcast), the SM handles it efficiently. The instruction sequence for one kh,kw iteration is: 128 FMAs interleaved with 128 shared memory loads. Shared memory latency is ~20 cycles. The GPU issues instructions for multiple warps simultaneously. With 8 warps, each warp's instructions can be pipelined to hide latency. Overall, this design should work well. assistantLet me analyze this problem and build an optimized fused CUDA kernel. Key observations: 1. Depthwise-separable conv = depthwise conv (groups=in_channels) + 1×1 pointwise conv 2. Fusing both operations avoids writing the full intermediate tensor to HBM 3. With batch=16, in_C=64, out_C=128, H=W=512, K=3, this is compute-bound (~37B FMAs) 4. Each output pixel requires computing a depthwise value per input channel, then combining across all output channels via pointwise weights My strategy: - Use shared memory for input tiles and broadcast pointwise weights - Each block handles a 32×8 spatial tile and 128 output channels - Template on kernel size K for loop unrolling - Precompute combined bias (pointwise(depthwise_bias) + pointwise_bias) Let me write and compile the kernel:
call → compile_kernel

(in_bounds) {\n#pragma unroll\n for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) {\n#pragma unroll\n for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) {\n int sy = ty * stride + kh * dilation;\n int sx = tx * stride + kw * dilation;\n * s_pw[i];\n }\n }\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n \n if (in_bounds) {\n float* out_ptr = output + ((b_idx * out_C) + oc_base) * out_plane_size;\n#pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < OC_TILE; i++) reasoning
The issue is that the C++ declaration of `fused_conv_forward` needs to be in the `cpp_sources` so it's visible when pybind11 generates the wrapper. Let me restructure to put the torch function declaration in cpp_sources. Actually, looking at the build error more carefully: `main.cpp` had `fused_conv_forward` not declared in scope. The auto-generated `main.cpp` from load_inline with empty cpp_sources tries to forward declare and wrap functions from the functions list. But our function signature includes torch::Tensor types which need the proper headers. The proper way is to provide a C++ source with the function declaration, or use `cpp_sources` with the declaration while the implementation is in `cuda_sources`.

Wait, 64K registers means 65,536 registers. Each register holds 32 bits. So total register storage per SM = 65,536 × 4 = 262,144 bytes. Total: ~24,000 registers per block. Per SM: 65,536 registers → max 2 blocks per SM concurrently. That's reasonable. The depthwise dot product is: ```cpp double dw_acc = 0.0; for (kh, kw) { double in = (double)s_in[sy*smem_w + sx]; double dw = (double)dw_weight[...]; dw_acc += in * dw; } double pw = (double)s_pw[i]; accum_d[i] += dw_acc * pw; ``` At the end: ```cpp out_ptr[...] = (float)accum_d[i]; ``` This should be very accurate.
